  </div>
    <div class="elementListBrownSeparator"></div>



  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2304157272" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2304157272" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2304157272?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2018-shelf">2018-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=worldbuilding-sf">worldbuilding-sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2304157272" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2149893731931392823">I think I've just stumbled upon one of those "Important Works" I keep saying is so necessary. No UF fluff with magical women or post-apocalypse SF nonsense where it's mostly about shocking us about the brutality of man against woman. (It seems that's mostly what it is, these days.) <br/><br/>Indeed, what we've got here is a careful and complex study of all the gender roles turned on its head, slowly, surely, and irrevocably.<br/><br/>We have women getting the power to shock the living shit out of anyone and teach t</span>
  <span id="freeText2149893731931392823" style="display:none">I think I've just stumbled upon one of those "Important Works" I keep saying is so necessary. No UF fluff with magical women or post-apocalypse SF nonsense where it's mostly about shocking us about the brutality of man against woman. (It seems that's mostly what it is, these days.) <br /><br />Indeed, what we've got here is a careful and complex study of all the gender roles turned on its head, slowly, surely, and irrevocably.<br /><br />We have women getting the power to shock the living shit out of anyone and teach this power to any other woman. Throw that wrinkle in today's worldwide culture, and suddenly we have a really ugly war of the sexes on a scale that's horrifying. All the abuses of power, of violence, of insanity, is now going to be returned to the sender.<br /><br />I'll be honest, I loved the grand build-up of this reversal of power, thought the justice of it was delicious as hell. But it's another thing to see the justice turn to insanity. The practice of power for power's sake. To see women roaming the streets looking to abuse a stray man who was just looking for it, to have training camps to make women all stronger, to destroy anything that gets in their way.<br /><br />It still seems like justice. All those nasty patriarchies and misogynists getting their due was FUN. Until it wasn't. Until women become fully as bad as the men always were. Or as we assume men always were.<br /><br />The book also brings up the question of archeology in a wonderful way, fast-forwarding 5000 years into a culture where women have all the power and men are treated exactly the way women are treated today. With intellectual dishonesty, prejudice, ignorance, and the assumption that things were always as they were now, that men in armies are a joke or a sexual fantasy that could never have been true, like some of us may say Amazonians are today. That all the ancient statues destroyed depicting men as warriors back then must have been a grand joke or not what it seems. That the author, a man, ought to change his name to a woman's for the publication of the book to lend it credence.<br /><br />And this last bit happens throughout this novel, lending it an inevitable and horrific outlook on the nature of the real problem.<br /><br />It's not men. Or women. It's as Adler says. It's a POWER issue. Those with power abuse it. Those with power over others need to have someone to USE it on. This is the root of the disease.<br /><br />I've often thought and agreed with certain authors who bring up the possibility that women DID have the power in our ancient pasts, 45-55 thousand years ago, the statues being the only evidence I really have. I liked to daydream about what lost societies, full cultures, civilizations in our own deep pasts might have been like before time eroded everything. <br /><br />There really is no reason not to believe we've been through this exact same cycle many times, always suffering the same hubris and error in thinking. Power causes upheaval. The pendulum swings. The weak inherit the earth, and then the new weak inherit the earth, and then the new weak inherit the earth.<br /><br />It's humbling, this turning of the wheel. <br /><br />But back to the novel. It's extremely well written and painful to witness and thought-provoking and even delightful at times. It's also deep. Easily one of the very best novels of it's kind, being obviously gender-centric, sociological, and quite entertaining on the character level. The skein of power is almost secondary to the story. It's all about turning the tables. :)<br /><br />I totally recommend this book for everyone. Period. It's just that brilliant and should be required reading for anyone in the discussion of what it means to be a woman OR a man. Let's open our eyes! :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="2149893731931392823" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1741591103" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1741591103" />
    <a title="Jenna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38727943-jenna"><img alt="Jenna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451400366p2/38727943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1741591103?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenna" href="/user/show/38727943-jenna">Jenna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780316547611" href="/book/show/33641244-the-power">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38727943-jenna?shelf=book-of-the-month-club-selection">book-of-the-month-club-selection</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38727943-jenna?shelf=2017-reading-challenge">2017-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38727943-jenna?shelf=communes-cults-clubs-sects-etc">communes-cults-clubs-sects-etc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38727943-jenna?shelf=crossed-over-to-the-dark-side">crossed-over-to-the-dark-side</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38727943-jenna?shelf=dystopia-or-cli-fi">dystopia-or-cli-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38727943-jenna?shelf=depressing">depressing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38727943-jenna?shelf=supernatural">supernatural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38727943-jenna?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38727943-jenna?shelf=disliked">disliked</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1741591103" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2348083333320363872">This was sort of like: Put some elements of The Hunger Games, The Handmaid's Tale, and Star Wars in a cocktail shaker, toast to The Imminent Apocalypse, pour neat and extra dirty and drink - which sounds AWESOME, and also very apropos of the 2017 zeitgeist...yet....<br/><br/>I didn't love it. I admired it, to be sure, and it def left me totes curious about inquiring into this author's other works. But as many other eloquent reviewers have already noted, it left me wanting in a few key areas.<br/><br/>I'm all for su</span>
  <span id="freeText2348083333320363872" style="display:none">This was sort of like: Put some elements of The Hunger Games, The Handmaid's Tale, and Star Wars in a cocktail shaker, toast to The Imminent Apocalypse, pour neat and extra dirty and drink - which sounds AWESOME, and also very apropos of the 2017 zeitgeist...yet....<br /><br />I didn't love it. I admired it, to be sure, and it def left me totes curious about inquiring into this author's other works. But as many other eloquent reviewers have already noted, it left me wanting in a few key areas.<br /><br />I'm all for succinct novels these days and think most are too long - where the hell are the editors and THEIR Power? - but this, on the other hand, felt like a trilogy massively abridged into 300-odd pages.<br /><br />I also agree with other reviewers that some of the main characters were more interesting and fleshed out than others: too many perspectives were distributed between too few pages, so some characters got short shrift and some became stereotyped or simplified beyond the grasp of enduring interest.<br /><br />And finally - yes, I completely agree with other reviewers that it's unrealistic, and reductive, that every single woman in the world promptly responded in basically the exact same way to the massive global change in circumstances, regardless of the myriad variations in culture, religion, age, etc. This seemed kind of anti-feminist to me, actually, and sort of offensively essentialist on many levels. For me, this sort of defeated the purpose of the entire dystopian novel project. <br /><br />Because, I don't understand the point of going through the pains of constructing a dystopian novel if your ultimate purpose is just to say that, even with different people in power and different means as an end to that power, things would all wind up in exactly the same shitshow as they currently are. Isn't the whole beauty and, well, Power, of the dystopian format that, below the unrelenting grimness, there shimmer the faint vestiges of hope that things could be different, better? - that there is something still worth fighting for? - that it's not merely a matter of six of one, half dozen of the other? - that there are people out there willing and able to fight against all odds to stem and reverse the tide of the current fuckery at large?? I mean, sure, you could just go with the whole Absoute Power Corrupts Absolutely thing and leave it at that, and while there's some truth to this, I think there's a little more nuance to the story, and that little more nuance is a lot more interesting to consider. <br /><br />All that aside - this is an original and creative book, and especially if you are an admirer of YA, dystopian, or SciFi - (which I caution I am not; much of this book ran dangerously close for me to what my admired GR friend Debbie might refer to as "woo woo")* - do consider taking a look.<br /><br />UPDATE of 11/5/17: After consideration, I'm lowering the review to 2 stars to better reflect the level of reading enjoyment (or non-enjoyment, I guess). I admired the project but appreciated the actual product only to a 2.5 degree at most.<br /><br />* I mean seriously: as in, "parts of your bodily tissues are electrified and sentient and talking to you woo woo!" You've been warned.</span>

<div id="review_1854767076" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1854767076" />
    <a title="Belinda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7120748-belinda"><img alt="Belinda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1323142548p2/7120748.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1854767076?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Belinda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Belinda" href="/user/show/7120748-belinda">Belinda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1854767076" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8098197681865867518">This was another of those books reviewed on the radio and given loads of positive hype. I love sci-fi, I love Margaret Atwood (Alderman's mentor), and I'm a feminist. I wanted to read this book because what could go wrong?<br/><br/>The first 20 chapters seemed to be ok. Naomi can write, well, of course she can, and the premise was sound. Women have been altered genetically by pollutants and have developed a skein. This enables them to generate electricity. Suddenly they are the more powerful sex.<br/><br/>We follow</span>
  <span id="freeText8098197681865867518" style="display:none">This was another of those books reviewed on the radio and given loads of positive hype. I love sci-fi, I love Margaret Atwood (Alderman's mentor), and I'm a feminist. I wanted to read this book because what could go wrong?<br /><br />The first 20 chapters seemed to be ok. Naomi can write, well, of course she can, and the premise was sound. Women have been altered genetically by pollutants and have developed a skein. This enables them to generate electricity. Suddenly they are the more powerful sex.<br /><br />We follow three women - Roxy, Mother Eve, and Margot - and 1 man - Tunde - as the world changes. Tunde is a journalist who chronicles demonstrations, political changes, and the uprising of men who hate their secondary status. Roxy is a woman who has the most powerful skein out there. Mother Eve, originally Allie, grows up in foster care and is abused until she murders Mr whoever and has to run away and create a new identity. Margot's a politician who is at the forefront of helping to empower women by providing them with classes on how to manage their "gift".<br /><br />Things start to go pear shaped when power goes to everyone's heads. The sort of brutality that women have to currently put up with all the time, the sexism, the put downs, rape, pain, torture, murder, now happens to men. Often the men seek it because it enhances sex, but women go crazy, especially once Glitter, a drug that makes women's electrical charges more powerful, comes along.<br /><br />It all sounds ok, right? Well, it isn't. I don't care one bit about Alderman's link with Atwood, her ability to write a good sentence, or the fact this book has sold and sold and sold, been broadcast on BBC radio 4, and will probably end up as a movie. It's sick. I get that the world would alter considerably if women became the big kahunas. I get that things could go wrong. I also understand that there could be a real backlash against men if women suddenly became so strong that they could do whatever they wanted to a man, but I don't believe women would become so feral and so insanely cruel.<br /><br />To me this book undermines women because it seems to say, "If we had The Power we'd rape men, kill them, physically pull them to pieces, because we can't act humanely." To me it says that even if women were physically strong we're mentally and emotionally too weak to do the right thing. Sure, this is fiction, but it's so gratuitously violent (wait until the scene where the women murder two tiny children hiding in a metal drum) that it is insulting and repulsive.<br /><br />The best books manage to show the many faces of humanity. Women are seen as nurturers, creators, more emotionally expressive, as well as smart, funny, moody, and so on. But in The Power they act like beasts. Even Margot, mother of Jocelyn, is totally selfish.<br /><br />What a horrible book this is. I wanted to give it no stars to express how much I ended up hating it. Read it if you want, but don't say I didn't warn you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8098197681865867518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2479508786" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2479508786" />
    <a title="Susanne  Strong" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4514828-susanne-strong"><img alt="Susanne  Strong" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583087410p2/4514828.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2479508786?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susanne  Strong" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susanne  Strong" href="/user/show/4514828-susanne-strong">Susanne  Strong</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4514828-susanne-strong?shelf=2018-five-star-books">2018-five-star-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4514828-susanne-strong?shelf=must-read">must-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4514828-susanne-strong?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2479508786" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12895135825494895899"><b>5 Completely Rad and Rockin Stars. <br/><br/>There are some novels that you feel, from the tips of your fingers, all the way to the bottoms of your toes. This is one of them. <br/><br/>All at once, something happens, something strange, something inexplicable and yet, one by one.. every teenage girl experiences it. A feeling simply overtakes them and they strike out at everyone who has wronged them. </b><br/><br/><blockquote> <i> She feels the thing like pins and needles along her arms. Like needle-pricks of light from her spine to her collarbo</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText12895135825494895899" style="display:none"><b>5 Completely Rad and Rockin Stars. <br /><br />There are some novels that you feel, from the tips of your fingers, all the way to the bottoms of your toes. This is one of them. <br /><br />All at once, something happens, something strange, something inexplicable and yet, one by one.. every teenage girl experiences it. A feeling simply overtakes them and they strike out at everyone who has wronged them. </b><br /><br /><blockquote> <i> She feels the thing like pins and needles along her arms. Like needle-pricks of light from her spine to her collarbone, from her throat to her elbows, wrists, to the pads of her fingers. She's glittering, inside.</i></blockquote> <br /><br />What can she do with it you ask? She touches you: a slight touch to the wrist and an electric current flows from her body to yours and her body awakens, she feels wholly alive. And you? You are in pain and you cannot move. It surges through you like a hot flame, rippling down your forearm, searing your skin, crackling and yet you can’t help but feel the excitement building, even if “The Power” kills you. <br /><br />When girls come into their power, it is visible to all, through a skein on their necks. A movement is on the rise, in the good ole’ US of A and across the World. What starts out as girls trying to stop boys from overstepping, from harassing them, becomes something else entirely. <br /><br />Allie’s home life was less than stellar, yet she escaped and now she takes care of those who can’t take care of themselves. Now known as Mother Eve, she oversees everyone and everything. <br /><br />Roxy is a gal with sass. Her family is unlike most: they are London gangstas. She is the best and brightest and her abilities are unmatched. If you aren’t with her, you’re against her. <br /><br />Tunde is a photographer. Once things go haywire.. bzzzt...his photos go national and he boldly follows the stories no one else dares. <br /><br />Margot is a politician and she would like to believe that she’s making a difference in these trying times even though her own daughter Jocelyn struggles more than most. Margot’s arrogant, egotistical and unqualified male boss (who kind of reminded me of someone..) is planning to run for re-election which ends up igniting a fire in Margot too. <br /><br />Their paths converge at different points in time and after that, well, all hell breaks loose. <br /><br /><b>At times, I shifted, edgy, uncomfortable, eyes wide - amazed. Then, unexpectedly, the corners of my mouth would haphazardly curve into a smile, in complete bewildered awe. Then I was horrified at myself for my inability to control my own reactions and scared by what was transpiring, I simply shuddered. “The Power” is transformative, in more ways than one. </b> There is one scene, that I cannot unsee, one scene, that I admit got to me. And yet, it is sheer brilliance. I cannot help but be astounded. <br /><br /><b>If the dominant roles shifted and girls and women ruled the world, what would happen? Would boys and better yet, men, submit or would they fight back? </b> Hmm.. food for thought, especially now, given the state of this nation. The parallels to today’s political climate were glaringly obvious and brilliant to boot.<br /><br /><b>Kudos to Naomi Alderman for her foresight, her storytelling and her amazing characters. This novel rocked my world and definitely belongs on my favorites shelf for 2018 and is one I highly recommend if you’re not afraid of a little sizzle.</b><br /><br />Thank you to my friend Susan for the recommendation! You were right, I'm so glad I read this.<br /><br />Published on Goodreads, Amazon and Twitter on 8.24.18.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12895135825494895899" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1847164910" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1847164910" />
    <a title="Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas"><img alt="Thomas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567953607p2/2018505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1847164910?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas">Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Thomas by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/3081414-laura">Laura</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=feminism">feminism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=read-for-dmv-bookclub">read-for-dmv-bookclub</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1847164910" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2111600761193957117">I feel so sad giving this book such a low rating because it had so much potential. It follows three female-identifying characters and one male-identifying character who reside in a world where girls and women have the power to produce electricity and hurt, torture, and kill people. This newfound ability brings about an amalgamation of changes, including political power plays, shifts in male-female relationship dynamics, and the burning question of girls' and women's new place in society. <i>The Pow</i></span>
  <span id="freeText2111600761193957117" style="display:none">I feel so sad giving this book such a low rating because it had so much potential. It follows three female-identifying characters and one male-identifying character who reside in a world where girls and women have the power to produce electricity and hurt, torture, and kill people. This newfound ability brings about an amalgamation of changes, including political power plays, shifts in male-female relationship dynamics, and the burning question of girls' and women's new place in society. <i>The Power</i> portrays a host of challenging situations and scenes, including sexual assault and political warfare.<br /><br />This book had so much unfulfilled potential. I love the premise, because it invites so many nuanced questions and discussions within the realm of feminism. What are the implications of empowering women just so they act more like men? As <a href="https://organizingchange.org/patriarchy-persists-can-change/" rel="nofollow noopener">bell hooks writes</a>, patriarchy has no gender, so what happens when one gender (or sex) gains power over their oppressors? What are the psychological benefits and costs of having this power? Unfortunately, these questions are only explored in the most rudimentary of ways in <i>The Power</i>. Instead of using scenes, dialogue, and character development to flesh out this dystopia/utopia and explore these meaningful questions, Naomi Alderman focuses only on portraying violence and shallow character interactions. While violence has a place in this world for sure, the book could have featured so much more. The commentary on gender dynamics, too, mostly took form in pithy one-liners with gender reversals that did not relay much nuance or insight.<br /><br />My second main disappointment with <i>The Power</i> centers on its characters. They all felt one-note, interchangeable, and lacking in any depth that would motivate us to invest in them. Some of their perspectives felt too long and some felt too short. The pacing and organization of their respective sections made little logical sense. I wish Alderman had focused more on crafting these characters in a more comprehensive way so that readers could better connect to them.<br /><br />I hate to say this, but I would not recommend this book. If interested in feminist writing, for fiction I would recommend <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/970702334?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Handmaid's Tale</i>
</a> by Margaret Atwood or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1842921279?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Kindred</i>
</a> by Octavia Butler, and for nonfiction I would recommend <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/800953498?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Appetites</i>
</a> by Caroline Knapp or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1475715783?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>All About Love</i>
</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1005980600?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Will to Change</i>
</a> by bell hooks, just to name a few. While I think Alderman aimed for cultural diversity by including a Nigerian character and Moldova as a setting in <i>The Power</i>, this attempt fell flat, similar to the rest of the book. I appreciate Alderman for trying with this concept, at the very least.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2111600761193957117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2687364409" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2687364409" />
    <a title="Justin Tate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23082940-justin-tate"><img alt="Justin Tate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593157920p2/23082940.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2687364409?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Justin Tate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Justin Tate" href="/user/show/23082940-justin-tate">Justin Tate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2687364409" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18286273716871001403">Love the premise. Novels that explore power dynamics are often fascinating. This is like a globalized Lord of the Flies, with women instead of children. I've heard it described as a feminist novel, although I think part of the cleverness is the impossible-to-answer question of whether it's a feminist novel or not. On one hand it exposes the failures of men over history by turning the tables, but on the other - with women in charge, there's just as many wars, lies, oppression, murder and rape as </span>
  <span id="freeText18286273716871001403" style="display:none">Love the premise. Novels that explore power dynamics are often fascinating. This is like a globalized Lord of the Flies, with women instead of children. I've heard it described as a feminist novel, although I think part of the cleverness is the impossible-to-answer question of whether it's a feminist novel or not. On one hand it exposes the failures of men over history by turning the tables, but on the other - with women in charge, there's just as many wars, lies, oppression, murder and rape as there's been with men in power.<br /><br />To me, the clear theme is that power inevitably corrupts regardless of who has it. With that interpretation, I only see it as a feminist novel by the way it shows the drastic need for gender equality, or power neutrality. I couldn't find any evidence to suggest it effectively advocates for women receiving supernatural powers. Except there is that novel-within-a-novel layer (written by a "man") to further the debate.<br /><br />Now, all the positive aspects aside, I found the actual reading experience nightmarishly painful. The cast is made up entirely of disagreeable characters, which is fine, but I couldn't even love to hate them. There were also too many stories to tell. To truly deliver a global experience, we are thrust into melodrama on seemingly every continent. I wish we could have stayed still long enough to at least appreciate one aspect of an otherwise brilliant message.<br /><br />Worst of all, the writing came across as embarrassingly immature. Every character loves the "F" word and spouts it constantly. Much of the action is essentially written as "ZAP! BANG! FUCK! ZAP! STAB! ZAP! FUCK!" It was awkward and cringe-worthy throughout, and I found myself speed-reading through the worst paragraphs so I could finally get to the end.<br /><br />OVERALL: Despite having a great message on power and corruption, the delivery was such a disappointment I wish I hadn't wasted my time. Oh well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18286273716871001403" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2286842265" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2286842265" />
    <a title="Christina - Recipe &amp; a Read" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24156068-christina---recipe-a-read"><img alt="Christina - Recipe &amp; a Read" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580849831p2/24156068.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2286842265?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christina - Recipe &amp; a Read" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christina - Recipe &amp; a Read" href="/user/show/24156068-christina---recipe-a-read">Christina - Recipe &amp; a Read</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2286842265" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8956933726365718321"><b>2.5, well I hyped this up in my head way more than I should have stars!!</b><br/><br/><i>Full review featured on my blog <a href='https://recipeandaread.wordpress.com/2019/04/10/review-the-power-by-naomi-alderman/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Recipe & a Read</a>!</i><br/><br/>Across the globe, young girls are waking up with unimaginable, unexplained power. With the touch of their hand they’re able to inflict searing, indescribable pain to the point of even death. As girls the world over are discovering this newly awakened power, that has been dormant in women for as long as we can remember, they also discover they’re able to “awaken” the power in th</span>
  <span id="freeText8956933726365718321" style="display:none"><b>2.5, well I hyped this up in my head way more than I should have stars!!</b><br /><br /><i>Full review featured on my blog <a href="https://recipeandaread.wordpress.com/2019/04/10/review-the-power-by-naomi-alderman/" rel="nofollow noopener">Recipe &amp; a Read</a>!</i><br /><br />Across the globe, young girls are waking up with unimaginable, unexplained power. With the touch of their hand they’re able to inflict searing, indescribable pain to the point of even death. As girls the world over are discovering this newly awakened power, that has been dormant in women for as long as we can remember, they also discover they’re able to “awaken” the power in the older generations as well. The implications of this shift to the power structure of the world are numerous. Governments, the media, society itself begins changing rapidly as women begin to exercise this power more and more. No longer are women afraid to walk the streets alone, but men must now be looking over their shoulder and preparing instead.<br /><br />As power shifts from men to women, it begs the big questions: What if women were in power? What if women were the “stronger” and more “powerful” sex? How would this affect our world and what would it look like if women were thrust into running the world? The Power is told from alternating POVs between numerous young girls coming into their new found skills, and a young man who captures some of the first glimpses of women the world over exercising this change in gender dynamics.<br /><br /><b>The shape of power is always the same: it is infinite, it is complex, it is forever branching. </b><br /><br />Well, I put a lot of hype into this book in my head and so maybe my disappointment is my own fault. Regardless of how much I built this novel up, it really let me down. For me, it’s always the worst when I feel like a story has so much promise and then falls flat with it’s lofty goals. The Power was such a unique take on a old tale that I felt like it was bound to knock me off my chair, ask important questions and challenge my view of the world. What started out incredibly strong, interesting and full of unique vision ultimately fell flat and completely missed the mark for me personally.<br /><br />I’ve considered myself a pretty staunch feminist for the majority of my life. Since “feminist” is sometimes considered a bad word, I’ve also spent a good deal of time explaining what feminism actually is and what it is not. What it is not, is men hating or wanting women to treat men the way men have treated women – which is oppression. There’s a great Chinese proverb that says “a candle loses nothing by lighting another candle” and that’s something that has always spoken to me. Women do not want a world in which they can treat men the way they’ve been treated, it’s simply about fighting the patriarchy to have equal representation. The Power really jumbled that message for me.<br /><br /><b>It doesn’t matter that she shouldn’t, that she never would. What matters is that she could, if she wanted. The power to hurt is a kind of wealth.</b><br /><br />Now, I’m not saying that there isn’t a chance that women could come into some kind of latent power and then become violent and oppressive. That’s definitely a possibility, but it’s reductive to me. Women have been fighting for equal rights, equal representation, equal pay for so long that I would’ve preferred to see women liberated instead of high on power. I also found it unrealistic that Islamic women, after an entire history of oppression begin ripping off their traditional garb and mobbing in the streets. As more and more girls come into this power, society completely collapses into a sea of gratuitous violence and still, a lot of sexism. Yes, women are the one’s in power but how is it that as feminists we can spend all this time talking about wanting to see a world free of sexism while simultaneously plugging books that promote sexism? This didn’t feel like a “win” for women to me.<br /><br />Past the larger, more theological issues I had with this book I also found that it really dragged for me after about the first quarter. The set up and the premise were incredibly interesting, I was originally semi-invested in some of the characters and how their stories would develop. However, as time went on I found a lot of the POVs to be redundant and best and a snore-fest at worst. There was too much time spent developing the violence and getting back at men, and not enough time developing the characters and their place within the overall story.<br /><br /><b>Gender is a shell game. What is a man? Whatever a woman isn’t. What is a woman? Whatever a man is not. Tap on it and it’s hollow. Look under the shells: it’s not there.</b><br /><br />As I’ve mentioned before I don’t normally post quotes that I specifically didn’t like, but what is this even saying? How does this even relate to the story? A lot of this read like it was trying incredibly hard to be deep and philosophical and it totally missed the mark for me. In the end, this book was not for me. It was a total failure or loss because the premise was absolutely unique and very interesting. I continued to read on in hopes that things would improve but I was ultimately let down.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8956933726365718321" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2061601610" />
    <a title="monica kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/748566-monica-kim"><img alt="monica kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545978883p2/748566.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2061601610?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="monica kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="monica kim" href="/user/show/748566-monica-kim">monica kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer3709180920246936801">"Are patriarchies peaceful because men are peaceful? Or do more peaceful societies tend to allow men to rise up to the top because they place less value on the capacity for violence?" ponders a male historian living in a matriarchal world where women hold the power. <br/><br/>This book doesn't just flip gender roles.   <span id="freeText3709180920246936801" style="display:none">"Are patriarchies peaceful because men are peaceful? Or do more peaceful societies tend to allow men to rise up to the top because they place less value on the capacity for violence?" ponders a male historian living in a matriarchal world where women hold the power. <br /><br />This book doesn't just flip gender roles. It delves into complicated discussions around systemic oppression, power, rape culture, gender, and religion. The book is an unflinching dystopian yet also a mirror of our world today. It forces you to ask hard questions about your beliefs. It's also a ridiculously gripping story that had me sucked in and invested in every character. <br /><br />EVERYONE should read this book. I am still reeling from it and am going to need some time to sit with it to get out my full thoughts together.<br /><br />Major TW for rape and assault.</span>

<div id="review_3432341276" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Sandra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/81046856-sandra"><img alt="Sandra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1524559703p2/81046856.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3432341276?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sandra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sandra" href="/user/show/81046856-sandra">Sandra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/81046856-sandra?shelf=current-lit-uk">current-lit-uk</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3432341276" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7926275731704857879">The Power by Naomi Alderman. What would you do if you were young and started developing a strange power that was beyond your control?<br/>This is what is happening to teenage girls all over the world. They find they have a prominent skein across their collar bone that can discharge an electrical current that can causes intense pain and even death to others. As they learn to control this strange phenomenon they find they can wake this dormant power in older women. <br/>We follow a number of people....... A</span>
  <span id="freeText7926275731704857879" style="display:none">The Power by Naomi Alderman. What would you do if you were young and started developing a strange power that was beyond your control?<br />This is what is happening to teenage girls all over the world. They find they have a prominent skein across their collar bone that can discharge an electrical current that can causes intense pain and even death to others. As they learn to control this strange phenomenon they find they can wake this dormant power in older women. <br />We follow a number of people....... Allie running from foster carers in America, voices in her head showing her the way forward. Roxy in England, seeing her mum murdered, taking her revenge and going on the run. <br />Margo a politician trying to help young girls control their changing bodies, setting up North Star training camps. Her daughter Jocelyn struggling with her power - she is shunned for not being ‘normal’ as her skein has not developed fully and will not always work. <br />Tunde a young man who sees the opportunity to chronicle the changing world, travelling, experiencing, filming, his outlet the media or his own social network.<br />The women protest, riot, hit back at the male governments, police force and army. They are the strong ones... they have the power! The world shifts and changes. <br />I loved the challenging thought provoking feel of the book and the religious quotes....God is She ! <br />The plot of the book is a book within a book. A male author is writing a book to try and show how the world was hundreds of years ago, his editor says he books is good but can he expect people to really believe that men once ran the world, caused wars and were warriors? The ending was subtle and funny....spot on 😊<br />The Power won the Baileys Women's Prize for Fiction in 2017.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7926275731704857879" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2255309693" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2255309693" />
    <a title="Heidi The Reader" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7986781-heidi-the-reader"><img alt="Heidi The Reader" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593309467p2/7986781.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2255309693?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Heidi The Reader" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Heidi The Reader" href="/user/show/7986781-heidi-the-reader">Heidi The Reader</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7986781-heidi-the-reader?shelf=feminism">feminism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7986781-heidi-the-reader?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7986781-heidi-the-reader?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7986781-heidi-the-reader?shelf=fantasy-and-sci-fi">fantasy-and-sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2255309693" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12861414758527031997">In <i>The Power</i>, young women have developed the ability to control electricity. It shifts the balance of power between the sexes and the world begins to come apart at the seams.<br/><br/>It is told from the point of view of a few women and a man. They each have different stories and experiences that Naomi Alderman blends together to create a powerful statement about how we live.<br/><br/>This is one of the most disturbing books I've ever read, but also, most brilliant. It made me think about all of the internal biases</span>
  <span id="freeText12861414758527031997" style="display:none">In <i>The Power</i>, young women have developed the ability to control electricity. It shifts the balance of power between the sexes and the world begins to come apart at the seams.<br /><br />It is told from the point of view of a few women and a man. They each have different stories and experiences that Naomi Alderman blends together to create a powerful statement about how we live.<br /><br />This is one of the most disturbing books I've ever read, but also, most brilliant. It made me think about all of the internal biases I have when it comes to gender, cultural expectations and roles.<br /><br />Who was it who said: "Absolute power corrupts absolutely." This book is an examination of power and how it has shaped the world, not always for the better.<br /><br />The monumental societal shift starts out small enough. A man named Tunde captures a moment between a young woman and a man who was hitting on her at the grocery store: <i>"Tunde is recording when she turns around. ... There she is, bringing her hand to his arm when he smiles and thinks she is performing mock-fury for his amusement. If you pause the video for a moment at this point, you can see the charge jump."</i> loc 261, ebook.<br /><br />Those who have been abused are more likely to become abusers. And there are many, many abused women in the world.<br /><br /><i>"A strange new kind of fighting which leaves boys- mostly boys, sometimes girls- breathless and twitching, with scars like unfurling leaves winding up their arms or legs or across the soft flesh of their middles. Their first thought after disease is a new weapon, something these kids are bringing into school, but as the first week trickles into the second they know that's not it."</i> loc 316, ebook.<br /><br />Entire governments crumble to powerful women. Women who have been locked up their entire lives roam the streets, free. Soon enough, they're locking up and abusing the men, because they can.<br /><br />Religions change. Sexual predilections change. New politicians are elected. New soldiers are trained.<br /><br /><i>"Allie thinks, God is telling the world that there is to be a new order. That the old way is overturned. The old centuries are done."</i> loc 681, ebook.<br /><br />The new scourge of third world countries are powerful, uncontrolled women.<br /><br /><i>"He wounds three of the women in the leg or arm and the others are on him like a tide. There is a sound like eggs frying. When Tunde gets close enough to show what has been done, he is perfectly still, the twisted-vine marks across his face and neck so thick that his features are barely discernible."</i> loc 884, ebook.<br /><br />I think book clubs may find plenty to talk about in this book- if they can make it through. There are some very disturbing scenes.<br /><br /><i>"Gender is a shell game. What is a man? Whatever a woman isn't. What is a woman? Whatever a man is not. Tap on it and it's hollow. Look under the shells: it's not there."</i> loc 4780, ebook.<br /><br />In a time when so many women may feel powerless or voiceless, <i>The Power</i> may speak directly to them. It is, as I've said, a disturbing book, but also a conversation starter.<br /><br />To quote Victor Hugo: <i>Nothing is as powerful as an idea whose time has come.</i> And, in my mind, it was the perfect time for this book to be written.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12861414758527031997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3243337418" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3243337418" />
    <a title="Elle (ellexamines)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52545435-elle-ellexamines"><img alt="Elle (ellexamines)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582250895p2/52545435.jpg" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3243337418?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elle (ellexamines)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elle (ellexamines)" href="/user/show/52545435-elle-ellexamines">Elle (ellexamines)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=y-out2016">y-out2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=2-star">2-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=2020reads">2020reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=sff-scifi-dystopias">sff-scifi-dystopias</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3243337418" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10289715256246682911">One morning, women all over the world begin developing a power: electricity, running through their fingers. The ensuing cataclysm, framed by documentation from the future, comes from four characters. There’s Roxy, 15-year-old white British daughter of a gangster; Tunde, a Nigerian journalist and the only man who narrates; Margot, an American mayor who develops the power despite her age; and Allie, a mixed-race abuse survivor who is stronger than normal in her power. As nations are conquered by w</span>
  <span id="freeText10289715256246682911" style="display:none">One morning, women all over the world begin developing a power: electricity, running through their fingers. The ensuing cataclysm, framed by documentation from the future, comes from four characters. There’s Roxy, 15-year-old white British daughter of a gangster; Tunde, a Nigerian journalist and the only man who narrates; Margot, an American mayor who develops the power despite her age; and Allie, a mixed-race abuse survivor who is stronger than normal in her power. As nations are conquered by women, things fall further and further apart. <br /><br />This book primarily works as <b>a subtle reverse of gendered power dynamics</b>. Tunde’s first scene, and some further, are <i>really</i> strong. Tunde’s role in the book, specifically, feels like a specific reversal of the roles we're used to seeing women and men play in literature. His experience in the introduction to the book is an excellent metaphor for subtler forms of sexual violence and turns your expectations for the situation on their head very quickly. <br /><br />This at first was really interesting to me. Over time, I realized it was all this book had to offer. Around 85%, I was seriously considering giving this book one star. <br /><br />The characters here generally left me colder. Tunde was the only narrator in this book I actually consistently <i>liked</i>, though Roxy grew on me a lot by the end. What bugged me about the characterization in general, though, was how much the characterization seemed to serve as a <b>vehicle for the plot</b>. Margot’s motivations and personality fall off the track on about her second chapter and never return. Roxy is a solid narrator, but I never felt like I <i>understood</i> her. Tunde’s only stated motivation remains his newfound ambition to <i>know</i>, and we don’t get any sense of where that comes from, making it difficult to invest in. Allie’s motivations, criminally, remain fairly murky, indeed coming about primarily as a voice in her head. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[We don’t see her challenge this voice, at all, even in the barest sense, until her last chapter; the audience, meanwhile, knows the voice is badly intentioned very quickly. A lot of buildup would’ve been needed to justify her never questioning the voice, and the book does not give that. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />As the characters are clearly not meant to be the focus here, the main interest should come in the storylines. Unfortunately that was not working for me, either. Past the halfway point, I struggled to stay invested in the narrative. Some of this may also be the writing’s at-times overdramatic quality; however, I really don’t think that’s the main concern here. Something about the storytelling here feels far more focused on <b>the brutality</b> of the moment, the violence inflicted, then on the human element behind it. <br /><br />This specifically began to bother me in terms of <b>sexual violence</b>. There are at least four graphic rape or attempted rape scenes in this novel (excluding the many instances of sexual harassment or threatened assault). The first one made me wince, but reminded me of the flipping of the targets of sexual violence; I especially appreciated the fact that the perpetrator invoked ‘he was asking for it’ as justification. The second one made me wince a bit more. The third struck me as genuinely gratuitous. By the fourth, which is also simultaneously a murder scene, I was really really struggling to not put the book away. <br /><br />We’ve all read novels by men who immediately jump to graphic sexual violence when they have no idea what to use as plot. I would like to think, on a meta level, that the author is commenting on this tendency. However, my feelings on the actual plot device—profoundly negative—do not change when the victims are men. <br /><br />The author also seems to have not taken into account essentially <i>any</i> of the nuances of gender discrimination based on culture, or based on <b>intersections with race</b>, sexuality, or gender identity. The fact that the societies we see break down are almost entirely non-European, while America and Britain both stay intact, began needling at me a <i>lot</i> as the brutality increased. I would enjoy seeing the takes of Middle Eastern or African reviewers on this. It’s also interesting that the author seems to not have taken into account variations on gender identity beyond ‘cis man’ and ‘cis woman’ (one intersex character appears, but no mention of trans people) in a novel about gender as a construct. <br /><br />The thing that worked for me the <i>most</i> about this book was actually, weirdly enough, the framing device. The idea that this book is actually a male author’s book about a far-back time in history, framed by his deferential letters with a female colleague, spoke volumes. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The line “This can’t have happened to women in the time before the cataclysm” is genuinely brilliant, and the line about “the canon of men’s fiction” really really <i>really</i> struck me. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> It may not have been enough to make this book a favorite, but it certainly left me with less of a bitter taste in my mouth. I only wish the rest of the book had worked so well. <br /><br />TW: rape (graphic), sexual harassment, abuse.<br /><br /><br /><a href="https://ellexamines.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/ellexamines" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="https://www.instagram.com/ellexamines/" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://sptfy.com/4F6I" rel="nofollow noopener">Spotify</a> | <a href="https://www.youtube.com/c/ellexamines" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a> | <a href="https://ellexamines.carrd.co/#about" rel="nofollow noopener">About</a> |</span>
  <a data-text-id="10289715256246682911" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1880531-carol-bookaria?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1880531-carol-bookaria?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2381189065" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6268472984031895947">This is an <b>insightful, fictional exploration of shifts of power </b>and the repercussions that a such sudden and dramatic change can have in society.<br/><br/>I am absolutely <b>amazed by this work</b> by Naomi Alderman, this is the first book I read from her and definitely won't be the last.<br/><br/>In the story, women in contemporary times develop the ability to emit electricity through their hands which can be used to physically hurt other people, they call this ability <b>"the power"</b>. As you can imagine, some of them start </span>
  <span id="freeText6268472984031895947" style="display:none">This is an <b>insightful, fictional exploration of shifts of power </b>and the repercussions that a such sudden and dramatic change can have in society.<br /><br />I am absolutely <b>amazed by this work</b> by Naomi Alderman, this is the first book I read from her and definitely won't be the last.<br /><br />In the story, women in contemporary times develop the ability to emit electricity through their hands which can be used to physically hurt other people, they call this ability <b>"the power"</b>. As you can imagine, some of them start zapping personas non-grata right away and that's all I want to share about the premise.<br /><br />The story is told from the points of view of four people, Roxy, Tunde, Allie, and Margot, they are characters with different backgrounds and paths in life.<br /><br />My <b>favorite part is the ending</b> which left me reeling. <br /><br />Overall, the story was <b>deeply incisive and imaginative</b>, I highly recommend it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6268472984031895947" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('1IbS4MB7ab3BSzCUeQzQU/mCYEskgbmWqazGY+XvGT6kQ29pDFR3yyR+hoxaRxE/DfzPs951Gn7oqBmB1alLSg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('StmQF5dfCJa0NStUxyTLeE5xDpskwXiKAjDteETentc6HC2eW3AW4FEAnUzkbwoUug+hY94122JDNDKadJjMow==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/118226.Naomi_Alderman">About Naomi Alderman</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/118226.Naomi_Alderman"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1272068831p3/118226.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16259907803374486723">Naomi Alderman (born 1974 in London) is a British author and novelist.<br/><br/>Alderman was educated at South Hampstead High School and Lincoln College, Oxford where she read Philosophy, Politics and Economics. She then went on to study creative writing at the University of East Anglia before becoming a novelist.<br/>She was the lead writer for Perplex City, an Alternate reality game, at Mind Candy from 2004 th</span>
  <span id="freeText16259907803374486723" style="display:none">Naomi Alderman (born 1974 in London) is a British author and novelist.<br /><br />Alderman was educated at South Hampstead High School and Lincoln College, Oxford where she read Philosophy, Politics and Economics. She then went on to study creative writing at the University of East Anglia before becoming a novelist.<br />She was the lead writer for Perplex City, an Alternate reality game, at Mind Candy from 2004 through June, 2007.[1]<br />Her father is Geoffrey Alderman, an academic who has specialised in Anglo-Jewish history. She and her father were interviewed in The Sunday Times "Relative Values" feature on 11 February 2007.[2]<br /><br />Her literary debut came in 2006 with Disobedience, a well-received (if controversial) novel about a rabbi's daughter from North London who becomes a lesbian, which won her the 2006 Orange Award for New Writers.<br />Since its publication in the United Kingdom, it has been issued in the USA, Germany, Israel, Holland, Poland and France and is due to be published in Italy, Hungary and Croatia.<br />She wrote the narrative for The Winter House, an online, interactive yet linear short story visualized by Jey Biddulph. The project was commissioned by Booktrust as part of the Story campaign, supported by Arts Council England. [3]</span>
  <a data-text-id="16259907803374486723" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
